<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Anglicanism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The <b>Church of England</b> (<b>C of E</b>) is the <a href="/wiki/State_church" class="mw-redirect" title="State church">state church</a> of <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Eberle_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eberle-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fox_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fox-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferrante_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferrante-5">[5]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a> (currently <a href="/wiki/Justin_Welby" title="Justin Welby">Justin Welby</a>) is the most senior cleric, although the monarch is the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Governor_of_the_Church_of_England" title="Supreme Governor of the Church of England">supreme governor</a>. The Church of England is also the <a href="/wiki/Mother_church" title="Mother church">mother church</a> of the international <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Communion</a>. It traces its history to the Christian church recorded as existing in the Roman province of Britain by the third century, and to the 6th-century <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_mission" title="Gregorian mission">Gregorian mission</a> to <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kent" title="Kingdom of Kent">Kent</a> led by <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Canterbury" title="Augustine of Canterbury">Augustine of Canterbury</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-DetailedHistory_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DetailedHistory-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>The English church renounced <a href="/wiki/Papal" class="mw-redirect" title="Papal">papal</a> authority when <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">Henry&#160;VIII</a> failed to secure an <a href="/wiki/Annulment" title="Annulment">annulment</a> of his marriage to <a href="/wiki/Catherine_of_Aragon" title="Catherine of Aragon">Catherine of Aragon</a> in the 1530s.<sup id="cite_ref-KH8-RC-00_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KH8-RC-00-9">[9]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">English Reformation</a> accelerated under <a href="/wiki/Edward_VI_of_England" title="Edward VI of England">Edward&#160;VI</a>'s regents, before a brief <a href="/wiki/Second_Statute_of_Repeal" title="Second Statute of Repeal">restoration of papal authority</a> under <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">Queen Mary I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Spain" title="Philip II of Spain">King Philip</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Supremacy_1558" title="Act of Supremacy 1558">Act of Supremacy 1558</a> renewed the breach and the <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_Settlement" class="mw-redirect" title="Elizabethan Settlement">Elizabethan Settlement</a> charted a course enabling the English church to describe itself as both <a href="/wiki/Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic">Catholic</a> and Reformed:</p>
<ul>
<li><i>Catholic</i> in that it views itself as a part of the universal church of <a href="/wiki/Jesus_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Jesus Christ">Jesus Christ</a> in unbroken continuity with the early <a href="/wiki/Apostolic_Age" title="Apostolic Age">apostolic</a> church. This is expressed in its emphasis on the teachings of the early <a href="/wiki/Church_Fathers" title="Church Fathers">Church Fathers</a>, as formalised in the <a href="/wiki/Apostles%27_Creed" title="Apostles' Creed">Apostles'</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed" title="Nicene Creed">Nicene</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Athanasian_Creed" title="Athanasian Creed">Athanasian</a> creeds.<sup id="cite_ref-cofe.anglican.org_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cofe.anglican.org-10">[10]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Reformed</i> in that it has been shaped by some of the doctrinal principles of the 16th-century <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a>, in particular in the <a href="/wiki/Thirty-Nine_Articles_of_Religion" class="mw-redirect" title="Thirty-Nine Articles of Religion">Thirty-Nine Articles of Religion</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cofe.anglican.org_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cofe.anglican.org-10">[10]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>In the earlier phase of the <a href="/wiki/English_Reformation" title="English Reformation">English Reformation</a> there were both <a href="/wiki/List_of_Catholic_martyrs_of_the_English_Reformation" title="List of Catholic martyrs of the English Reformation">Catholic martyrs</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Protestant_martyrs_of_the_English_Reformation" title="List of Protestant martyrs of the English Reformation">radical Protestant</a> martyrs. The later phases saw the <a href="/wiki/English_Penal_Laws" class="mw-redirect" title="English Penal Laws">Penal Laws</a> punish Roman Catholic and <a href="/wiki/Nonconformist" title="Nonconformist">nonconforming Protestants</a>. In the 17th century, political and religious disputes raised the <a href="/wiki/Puritan" class="mw-redirect" title="Puritan">Puritan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterian">Presbyterian</a> faction to control of the church, but this ended with the <a href="/wiki/Restoration_(1660)" title="Restoration (1660)">Restoration</a>. Papal recognition of <a href="/wiki/George_III_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="George III of England">George&#160;III</a> in 1766 led to greater religious tolerance.</p>
<p>Since the English Reformation, the Church of England has used a <a href="/wiki/Liturgy" title="Liturgy">liturgy</a> in English. The church contains several doctrinal strands, the main three known as <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Catholic">Anglo-Catholic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Anglicanism" title="Evangelical Anglicanism">Evangelical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Broad_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Broad Church">Broad Church</a>. Tensions between theological conservatives and progressives find expression in debates over the <a href="/wiki/Ordination_of_women_in_the_Anglican_Communion" title="Ordination of women in the Anglican Communion">ordination of women</a> and <a href="/wiki/Homosexuality_and_Anglicanism" class="mw-redirect" title="Homosexuality and Anglicanism">homosexuality</a>. The church includes both <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Christianity" title="Liberal Christianity">liberal</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_Conservative_Evangelicalism" class="mw-redirect" title="British Conservative Evangelicalism">conservative</a> clergy and members.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>The governing structure of the church is based on <a href="/wiki/Diocese" title="Diocese">dioceses</a>, each presided over by a bishop. Within each diocese are local parishes. The <a href="/wiki/General_Synod_of_the_Church_of_England" title="General Synod of the Church of England">General Synod of the Church of England</a> is the legislative body for the church and comprises bishops, other clergy and <a href="/wiki/Laity" title="Laity">laity</a>. Its measures must be approved by both <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Houses of Parliament</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Church_of_England" title="History of the Church of England">History of the Church of England</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Christianity_in_England">Early Christianity in England</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early Christianity in England">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Christianity_in_England" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Christianity in England">History of Christianity in England</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_Christianity" title="Anglo-Saxon Christianity">Anglo-Saxon Christianity</a></div>
<p>According to tradition, Christianity arrived in Britain in the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_1st_century" title="Christianity in the 1st century">1st</a> or <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_2nd_century" title="Christianity in the 2nd century">2nd century</a>, during which time southern Britain became part of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>. The earliest historical evidence of Christianity among the native <a href="/wiki/Britons_(historical)" class="mw-redirect" title="Britons (historical)">Britons</a> is found in the writings of such early Christian Fathers as <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Origen" title="Origen">Origen</a> in the first years of the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_3rd_century" title="Christianity in the 3rd century">3rd century</a>. Three <a href="/wiki/Romano-British" class="mw-redirect" title="Romano-British">Romano-British</a> bishops, including <a href="/wiki/Restitutus" title="Restitutus">Restitutus</a>, are known to have been present at the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Arles" class="mw-redirect" title="Council of Arles">Council of Arles</a> in 314.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Others attended the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Sardica" class="mw-redirect" title="Council of Sardica">Council of Sardica</a> in 347 and that of <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Rimini" class="mw-redirect" title="Council of Rimini">Ariminum</a> in 360, and a number of references to the church in <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Roman Britain</a> are found in the writings of <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_the_4th_century" title="Christianity in the 4th century">4th century</a> Christian fathers. Britain was the home of <a href="/wiki/Pelagius" title="Pelagius">Pelagius</a>, who opposed <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a>'s doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Original_sin" title="Original sin">original sin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>While Christianity was long established as the religion of the Britons at the time of the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_invasion_of_Britain" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon invasion of Britain">Anglo-Saxon invasion</a>, Christian Britons made little progress in converting the newcomers from their <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_paganism" title="Anglo-Saxon paganism">native paganism</a>. Consequently, in 597, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_I" title="Pope Gregory I">Pope Gregory I</a> sent the <a href="/wiki/Prior" title="Prior">prior</a> of the <a href="/wiki/San_Gregorio_Magno_al_Celio" title="San Gregorio Magno al Celio">Abbey of St Andrew's</a> (later canonised as <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Canterbury" title="Augustine of Canterbury">Augustine of Canterbury</a>) from Rome to evangelise the <a href="/wiki/Angles" title="Angles">Angles</a>. This event is known as the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_mission" title="Gregorian mission">Gregorian mission</a> and is the date the Church of England generally marks as the beginning of its formal history. With the help of Christians already residing in <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kent" title="Kingdom of Kent">Kent</a>, Augustine established his church at <a href="/wiki/Canterbury" title="Canterbury">Canterbury</a>, the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kent" title="Kingdom of Kent">Kingdom of Kent</a>, and became the first in the series of <a href="/wiki/Archbishops_of_Canterbury" class="mw-redirect" title="Archbishops of Canterbury">Archbishops of Canterbury</a> in 598. A later archbishop, the Greek <a href="/wiki/Theodore_of_Tarsus" title="Theodore of Tarsus">Theodore of Tarsus</a>, also contributed to the organisation of Christianity in England. The Church of England has been in continuous existence since the days of St Augustine, with the Archbishop of Canterbury as its episcopal head. Despite the various disruptions of the Reformation and the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a>, the Church of England considers itself to be the same church which was more formally organised by Augustine.<sup id="cite_ref-DetailedHistory_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DetailedHistory-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>While some Celtic Christian practices were changed at the <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Whitby" title="Synod of Whitby">Synod of Whitby</a>, the Christian in the British Isles was under papal authority from earliest times.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Queen <a href="/wiki/Bertha_of_Kent" title="Bertha of Kent">Bertha of Kent</a> was among the Christians in England who recognised papal authority before Augustine arrived,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> and Celtic Christians were carrying out missionary work with papal approval long before the Synod of Whitby.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hereford_Cathedral_Interior_May_2004.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Hereford_Cathedral_Interior_May_2004.jpg/220px-Hereford_Cathedral_Interior_May_2004.jpg" width="220" height="338" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Hereford_Cathedral_Interior_May_2004.jpg/330px-Hereford_Cathedral_Interior_May_2004.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4c/Hereford_Cathedral_Interior_May_2004.jpg 2x" data-file-width="417" data-file-height="640" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hereford_Cathedral_Interior_May_2004.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Hereford_Cathedral" title="Hereford Cathedral">Hereford</a> is one of the church's 43 cathedrals; many have histories stretching back centuries</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Synod of Whitby established the Roman date for Easter and the Roman style of monastic tonsure in England. This meeting of the ecclesiastics with Roman customs with local bishops was summoned in 664 at Saint Hilda's double monastery of Streonshalh (Streanæshalch), later called <a href="/wiki/Whitby_Abbey" title="Whitby Abbey">Whitby Abbey</a>. It was presided over by <a href="/wiki/Oswiu" title="Oswiu">King Oswiu</a>, who did not engage in the debate but made the final ruling.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Separation_from_Rome">Separation from Rome</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Separation from Rome">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1534, <a href="/wiki/Henry_VIII_of_England" title="Henry VIII of England">King Henry VIII</a> separated the English Church from Rome.<sup id="cite_ref-KH8-RC-00_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KH8-RC-00-9">[9]</a></sup> A theological separation had been foreshadowed by various movements within the English Church, such as <a href="/wiki/Lollardy" title="Lollardy">Lollardy</a>, but the English Reformation gained political support when Henry VIII wanted an <a href="/wiki/Annulment" title="Annulment">annulment</a> of his marriage to <a href="/wiki/Catherine_of_Aragon" title="Catherine of Aragon">Catherine of Aragon</a> so he could marry <a href="/wiki/Anne_Boleyn" title="Anne Boleyn">Anne Boleyn</a>. <a href="/wiki/Pope_Clement_VII" title="Pope Clement VII">Pope Clement VII</a>, considering that the earlier marriage had been entered under a <a href="/wiki/Dispensation_(canon_law)" title="Dispensation (canon law)">papal dispensation</a> and how Catherine's nephew, <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">Emperor Charles V</a>, might react to such a move, refused the annulment. Eventually, Henry, although theologically opposed to Protestantism, took the position of <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Governor_of_the_Church_of_England" title="Supreme Governor of the Church of England">Supreme Head of the Church of England</a> to ensure the annulment of his marriage. He was <a href="/wiki/Excommunicated" class="mw-redirect" title="Excommunicated">excommunicated</a> by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_III" title="Pope Paul III">Pope Paul III</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1536–40 Henry VIII engaged in the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Monasteries" title="Dissolution of the Monasteries">Dissolution of the Monasteries</a>, which controlled much of the richest land. He disbanded <a href="/wiki/Monastery" title="Monastery">monasteries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Priory" title="Priory">priories</a>, <a href="/wiki/Convent" title="Convent">convents</a> and <a href="/wiki/Friary" class="mw-redirect" title="Friary">friaries</a> in England, Wales and Ireland, appropriated their income, disposed of their assets, and provided pensions for the former residents. The properties were sold to pay for the wars. Bernard argues:</p>
<dl>
<dd>The dissolution of the monasteries in the late 1530s was one of the most revolutionary events in English history. There were nearly 900 religious houses in England, around 260 for monks, 300 for regular canons, 142 nunneries and 183 friaries; some 12,000 people in total, 4,000 monks, 3,000 canons, 3,000 friars and 2,000 nuns....one adult man in fifty was in religious orders.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<p>Henry maintained a strong preference for traditional Catholic practices and, during his reign, Protestant reformers were unable to make many changes to the practices of the Church of England. Indeed, this part of Henry's reign saw the trial for heresy of Protestants as well as Roman Catholics.</p>
<p>Under his son, <a href="/wiki/Edward_VI_of_England" title="Edward VI of England">King Edward VI</a>, more Protestant-influenced forms of worship were adopted. Under the leadership of the Archbishop of Canterbury, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cranmer" title="Thomas Cranmer">Thomas Cranmer</a>, a more radical reformation proceeded. A new pattern of worship was set out in the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a> (1549 and 1552). These were based on the older liturgy but influenced by Protestant principles. The confession of the reformed Church of England was set out in the <a href="/wiki/Forty-two_Articles" class="mw-redirect" title="Forty-two Articles">Forty-two Articles</a> (later revised to thirty-nine). The reformation however was cut short by the death of the king. <a href="/wiki/Mary_I_of_England" title="Mary I of England">Queen Mary I</a>, who succeeded him, returned England again to the authority of the papacy, thereby ending the first attempt at an independent Church of England. During her co-reign with her husband, <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Spain" title="Philip II of Spain">King Philip</a>, many leaders and common people were burnt for their refusal to recant of their reformed faith. These are known as the <a href="/wiki/Marian_martyrs" class="mw-redirect" title="Marian martyrs">Marian martyrs</a> and the persecution led to her nickname of "Bloody Mary".</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rochester_cathedral_stained_glass_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Rochester_cathedral_stained_glass_2.jpg/220px-Rochester_cathedral_stained_glass_2.jpg" width="220" height="330" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Rochester_cathedral_stained_glass_2.jpg/330px-Rochester_cathedral_stained_glass_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Rochester_cathedral_stained_glass_2.jpg/440px-Rochester_cathedral_stained_glass_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="1536" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rochester_cathedral_stained_glass_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Stained glass window in <a href="/wiki/Rochester_Cathedral" title="Rochester Cathedral">Rochester Cathedral</a>, Kent</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Mary also died childless and so it was left to the new regime of her half-sister Elizabeth to resolve the direction of the church. The settlement under <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England" title="Elizabeth I of England">Queen Elizabeth I</a> (from 1558), known as the <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_Settlement" class="mw-redirect" title="Elizabethan Settlement">Elizabethan Settlement</a>, developed the <i>via media</i> (middle way) character of the Church of England, a church moderately Reformed in doctrine, as expressed in the <a href="/wiki/Thirty-Nine_Articles" class="mw-redirect" title="Thirty-Nine Articles">Thirty-Nine Articles</a>, but also emphasising continuity with the Catholic and Apostolic traditions of the <a href="/wiki/Church_Fathers" title="Church Fathers">Church Fathers</a>. It was also an <a href="/wiki/Established_church" class="mw-redirect" title="Established church">established church</a> (constitutionally established by the state with the head of state as its <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Governor_of_the_Church_of_England" title="Supreme Governor of the Church of England">supreme governor</a>). The exact nature of the relationship between church and state would be a source of continued friction into the next century.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stuart_period">Stuart period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Stuart period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Stuart_period" title="Stuart period">Stuart period</a></div>
<p>For the next century, through the reigns of <a href="/wiki/James_VI_and_I" title="James VI and I">James I</a>, who ordered the creation of what became known as the <a href="/wiki/Authorized_King_James_Version" class="mw-redirect" title="Authorized King James Version">King James Bible</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Charles I</a>, culminating in the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a> and the Protectorate of <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Cromwell" title="Oliver Cromwell">Oliver Cromwell</a>, there were significant swings back and forth between two factions: the <a href="/wiki/Puritans" title="Puritans">Puritans</a> (and other radicals) who sought more far-reaching Protestant reforms, and the more conservative churchmen who aimed to keep closer to traditional beliefs and Catholic practices. The failure of political and ecclesiastical authorities to submit to Puritan demands for more extensive reform was one of the causes of open warfare. By Continental standards, the level of violence over religion was not high, but the casualties included King Charles I and the Archbishop of Canterbury, <a href="/wiki/William_Laud" title="William Laud">William Laud</a>. Under the Commonwealth and the Protectorate of England from 1649 to 1660, the bishops were dethroned and former practices were outlawed, and <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_polity" title="Presbyterian polity">Presbyterian</a> ecclesiology was introduced in place of the episcopate. The 39 Articles were replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Confession" class="mw-redirect" title="Westminster Confession">Westminster Confession</a>, the Book of Common Prayer by the Directory of Public Worship. Despite this, about one quarter of English clergy refused to conform to this form of State Presbyterianism.</p>
<p>With the Restoration of Charles II, Parliament restored the Church of England to a form not far removed from the Elizabethan version. One difference was that the ideal of encompassing all the people of England in one religious organisation, taken for granted by the Tudors, had to be abandoned. The religious landscape of England assumed its present form, with the Anglican established church occupying the middle ground, and those Puritans and Protestants who <a href="/wiki/English_Dissenters" title="English Dissenters">dissented</a> from the Anglican establishment, and Roman Catholics, too strong to be suppressed altogether, having to continue their existence outside the national church rather than controlling it. Continuing official suspicion and legal restrictions continued well into the 19th century.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="19th_century">19th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 19th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By the Fifth Article of the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1800" title="Acts of Union 1800">Union with Ireland 1800</a>, the Church of England and <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Ireland" title="Church of Ireland">Church of Ireland</a> were united into "one Protestant Episcopal church, to be called, the United Church of England and Ireland".<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Although this union was declared "an essential and fundamental Part of the Union",<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Church_Act_1869" title="Irish Church Act 1869">Irish Church Act 1869</a> separated the Irish part of the church again and disestablished it, the Act coming into effect on 1 January 1871.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overseas_developments">Overseas developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Overseas developments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Smith_1624_map_of_Bermuda_with_Forts_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/John_Smith_1624_map_of_Bermuda_with_Forts_01.jpg/200px-John_Smith_1624_map_of_Bermuda_with_Forts_01.jpg" width="200" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/John_Smith_1624_map_of_Bermuda_with_Forts_01.jpg/300px-John_Smith_1624_map_of_Bermuda_with_Forts_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/John_Smith_1624_map_of_Bermuda_with_Forts_01.jpg/400px-John_Smith_1624_map_of_Bermuda_with_Forts_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1370" data-file-height="1073" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Smith_1624_map_of_Bermuda_with_Forts_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Captain <a href="/wiki/John_Smith_of_Jamestown" class="mw-redirect" title="John Smith of Jamestown">John Smith's</a> 1624 map of Bermuda, showing St Peter's at centre, left</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Communion</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Historical_development_of_Church_of_England_dioceses#Colonial_dioceses" title="Historical development of Church of England dioceses">Historical development of Church of England dioceses §&#160;Colonial dioceses</a></div>
<p>As the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> expanded, British colonists and colonial administrators took the established church doctrines and practices together with ordained ministry and formed overseas branches of the Church of England. As they developed or, beginning with the United States of America, became sovereign or independent states, many of their churches became separate organisationally but remained linked to the Church of England through the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Communion</a>.</p>
<p>In Bermuda, the oldest remaining English colony (now designated a <a href="/wiki/British_Overseas_Territory" class="mw-redirect" title="British Overseas Territory">British Overseas Territory</a>), the first Church of England services were performed by the Reverend Richard Buck, one of the survivors of the 1609 wreck of the <i><a href="/wiki/Sea_Venture" title="Sea Venture">Sea Venture</a></i> which initiated Bermuda's permanent settlement. The nine parishes of the Church of England in <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermuda</a>, each with its own church and <a href="/wiki/Glebe" title="Glebe">glebe land</a>, rarely had more than a pair of ordained ministers to share between them until the Nineteenth Century. From 1825 to 1839, Bermuda's parishes were attached to the <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Nova_Scotia_and_Prince_Edward_Island" title="Diocese of Nova Scotia and Prince Edward Island">See of Nova Scotia</a>. Bermuda was then grouped into the new Diocese of Newfoundland and Bermuda from 1839. In 1879, the Synod of the Church of England in Bermuda was formed. At the same time, a Diocese of Bermuda became separate from the <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Newfoundland" title="Diocese of Newfoundland">Diocese of Newfoundland</a>, but both continued to be grouped under the <i>Bishop of Newfoundland and Bermuda</i> until 1919, when Newfoundland and Bermuda each received its own Bishop.</p>
<p>The Church of England in Bermuda was renamed in 1978 as the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Church_of_Bermuda" title="Anglican Church of Bermuda">Anglican Church of Bermuda</a>, which is an <a href="/wiki/Extra-provincial_Anglican_churches" title="Extra-provincial Anglican churches">extra-provincial diocese</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> with both <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_bishop" title="Metropolitan bishop">metropolitan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Primate_(bishop)" title="Primate (bishop)">primatial</a> authority coming directly from the Archbishop of Canterbury. Among its parish churches is <a href="/wiki/St._Peter%27s_Church,_St._George%27s" title="St. Peter's Church, St. George's">St Peter's Church</a> in the <a href="/wiki/UNESCO_World_Heritage_Site" class="mw-redirect" title="UNESCO World Heritage Site">UNESCO World Heritage Site</a> of <a href="/wiki/St._George%27s,_Bermuda" title="St. George's, Bermuda">St George's Town</a>, which is both the oldest Anglican and the oldest non-Roman Catholic church in the New World.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="21st_century">21st century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 21st century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Deposition_from_holy_orders_overturned">Deposition from holy orders overturned</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Deposition from holy orders overturned">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Under the guidance of <a href="/wiki/Rowan_Williams" title="Rowan Williams">Rowan Williams</a> and with significant pressure from clergy union representatives, the ecclesiastical penalty for convicted felons to be defrocked was set aside from the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Church_of_England_Measures" title="List of Church of England Measures">Clergy Discipline Measure 2003</a>. The clergy union argued that the penalty was unfair to victims of hypothetical miscarriages of criminal justice, because the ecclesiastical penalty is considered irreversible. Although clerics can still be banned for life from ministry, they remain ordained as priests.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dwindling_congregations">Dwindling congregations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Dwindling congregations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The archbishops of <a href="/wiki/Justin_Welby" title="Justin Welby">Canterbury</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Sentamu" title="John Sentamu">York</a> warned in January 2015 that the Church of England will no longer be able to carry on in its current form unless the downward spiral in membership is somehow reversed as typical Sunday attendances have halved to 800,000 in the last 40 years:<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The urgency of the challenge facing us is not in doubt. Attendance at Church of England services has declined at an average of one per cent per annum over recent decades and, in addition, the age profile of our membership has become significantly older than that of the population... Renewing and reforming aspects of our institutional life is a necessary but far from sufficient response to the challenges facing the Church of England... The age profile of our clergy has also been increasing. Around 40 per cent of parish clergy are due to retire over the next decade or so.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>However, <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Mullally" title="Sarah Mullally">Sarah Mullally</a>, the fourth woman chosen to become a bishop in the Church of England, insisted in June 2015 that declining numbers at services should not necessarily be a cause of despair for churches because people will still "encounter God" without ever taking their place in a pew, saying that people might hear the Christian message through social media sites such as Facebook or in a café run as a community project.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Additionally, the church's own statistics reveal that 9.7 million people visit an Anglican church every year and 1 million students are educated at Anglican schools.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Low_salaries">Low salaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Low salaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 2015 the Church of England admitted that it was embarrassed to be paying staff under the living wage. The Church of England had previously campaigned for all employers to pay this minimum amount. The archbishop acknowledged it was not the only area where the church "fell short of its standards".<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Doctrine_and_practice">Doctrine and practice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Doctrine and practice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anglican_doctrine" title="Anglican doctrine">Anglican doctrine</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hooker-Statue.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Hooker-Statue.jpeg/220px-Hooker-Statue.jpeg" width="220" height="262" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Hooker-Statue.jpeg/330px-Hooker-Statue.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/20/Hooker-Statue.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="477" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hooker-Statue.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Richard_Hooker_(theologian)" class="mw-redirect" title="Richard Hooker (theologian)">Richard Hooker</a> (1554–1600), one of the most influential figures in shaping Anglican theology and self-identity</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Canon_law" title="Canon law">canon law</a> of the Church of England identifies the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Christian scriptures</a> as the source of its doctrine. In addition, doctrine is also derived from the teachings of the <a href="/wiki/Church_Fathers" title="Church Fathers">Church Fathers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_councils" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecumenical councils">ecumenical councils</a> (as well as the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical_creeds" title="Ecumenical creeds">ecumenical creeds</a>) in so far as these agree with scripture. This doctrine is expressed in the <a href="/wiki/Thirty-Nine_Articles_of_Religion" class="mw-redirect" title="Thirty-Nine Articles of Religion">Thirty-Nine Articles of Religion</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a>, and the Ordinal containing the rites for the ordination of <a href="/wiki/Deacon#Anglicanism" title="Deacon">deacons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anglican_priest" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican priest">priests</a>, and the consecration of bishops.<sup id="cite_ref-CanonA5_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CanonA5-27">[27]</a></sup> Unlike other traditions, the Church of England has no single theologian that it can look to as a founder. However, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Hooker" title="Richard Hooker">Richard Hooker</a>'s appeal to scripture, <a href="/wiki/Christian_tradition" title="Christian tradition">church tradition</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a> as sources of authority continue to inform Anglican identity.<sup id="cite_ref-EncRelg349-350_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EncRelg349-350-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Canterbury_Cathedral_-_Portal_Nave_Cross-spire.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Canterbury_Cathedral_-_Portal_Nave_Cross-spire.jpeg/220px-Canterbury_Cathedral_-_Portal_Nave_Cross-spire.jpeg" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Canterbury_Cathedral_-_Portal_Nave_Cross-spire.jpeg/330px-Canterbury_Cathedral_-_Portal_Nave_Cross-spire.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Canterbury_Cathedral_-_Portal_Nave_Cross-spire.jpeg/440px-Canterbury_Cathedral_-_Portal_Nave_Cross-spire.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="3113" data-file-height="2395" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Canterbury_Cathedral_-_Portal_Nave_Cross-spire.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Canterbury_Cathedral" title="Canterbury Cathedral">Canterbury Cathedral</a> houses the <i><a href="/wiki/Cathedra" title="Cathedra">cathedra</a></i> or episcopal chair of the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a> and is the cathedral of the <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Canterbury" title="Diocese of Canterbury">Diocese of Canterbury</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mother_church" title="Mother church">mother church</a> of the Church of England as well as a focus for the <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion" title="Anglican Communion">Anglican Communion</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Church of England's doctrinal character today is largely the result of the Elizabethan Settlement, which sought to establish a comprehensive middle way between Roman Catholicism and Protestantism. The Church of England affirms the Protestant Reformation principle that scripture contains all things necessary to salvation and is the final arbiter in doctrinal matters. The Thirty-nine Articles are the church's only official confessional statement. Though not a complete system of doctrine, the articles highlight areas of agreement with <a href="/wiki/Lutheran" class="mw-redirect" title="Lutheran">Lutheran</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Reformed</a> positions, while differentiating Anglicanism from Roman Catholicism and <a href="/wiki/Anabaptism" title="Anabaptism">Anabaptism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-EncRelg349-350_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EncRelg349-350-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>While embracing some themes of the Protestant Reformation, the Church of England also maintains Catholic traditions of the ancient church and teachings of the Church Fathers, unless these are considered contrary to scripture. It accepts the decisions of the first four ecumenical councils concerning the <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">Trinity</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Incarnation_(Christianity)" title="Incarnation (Christianity)">Incarnation</a>. The Church of England also preserves Catholic order by adhering to <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_polity" title="Episcopal polity">episcopal polity</a>, with ordained orders of bishops, priests and deacons. There are differences of opinion within the Church of England over the necessity of episcopacy. Some consider it essential, while others feel it is needed for the proper ordering of the church.<sup id="cite_ref-EncRelg349-350_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EncRelg349-350-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Church of England has, as one of its distinguishing marks, a breadth and "open-mindedness". This tolerance has allowed Anglicans who emphasise the Catholic tradition and others who emphasise the Reformed tradition to coexist. The three "parties" (see <a href="/wiki/Churchmanship" title="Churchmanship">Churchmanship</a>) in the Church of England are sometimes called <a href="/wiki/High_church" title="High church">high church</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Catholic">Anglo-Catholic</a>), <a href="/wiki/Low_church" title="Low church">low church</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Anglican" class="mw-redirect" title="Evangelical Anglican">evangelical Anglican</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Broad_church" title="Broad church">broad church</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Christianity" title="Liberal Christianity">liberal</a>). The high church party places importance on the Church of England's continuity with the pre-Reformation Catholic Church, adherence to ancient liturgical usages and the sacerdotal nature of the priesthood. As their name suggests, Anglo-Catholics maintain many traditional Catholic practices and liturgical forms.<sup id="cite_ref-HighChurch_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HighChurch-29">[29]</a></sup> The low church party is more Protestant in both ceremony and theology.<sup id="cite_ref-LowChurch_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LowChurch-30">[30]</a></sup> Historically, broad church has been used to describe those of middle-of-the-road ceremonial preferences who lean theologically towards liberal Protestantism.<sup id="cite_ref-BroadChurch_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BroadChurch-31">[31]</a></sup> The balance between these strands of churchmanship is not static: in 2013, 40% of Church of England worshippers attended evangelical churches (compared with 26% in 1989), and 83% of very large congregations were evangelical. Such churches were also reported to attract higher numbers of men and young adults than others.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Worship_and_liturgy">Worship and liturgy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Worship and liturgy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Church of England's official book of liturgy as established in English Law is the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a></i>. In addition to this book the General Synod has also legislated for a modern <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_book" title="Liturgical book">liturgical book</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Common_Worship" title="Common Worship">Common Worship</a></i>, dating from 2000, which can be used as an alternative to the BCP. Like its predecessor, the 1980 <i><a href="/wiki/Alternative_Service_Book" title="Alternative Service Book">Alternative Service Book</a></i>, it differs from the <i>Book of Common Prayer</i> in providing a range of alternative services, mostly in modern language, although it does include some BCP-based forms as well, for example Order Two for Holy Communion. (This is a revision of the BCP service, altering some words and allowing the insertion of some other liturgical texts such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Agnus_Dei" class="mw-redirect" title="Agnus Dei">Agnus Dei</a></i> before communion.) The Order One rite follows the pattern of more modern liturgical scholarship.</p>
<p>The liturgies are organised according to the traditional <a href="/wiki/Liturgical_year" title="Liturgical year">liturgical year</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Calendar_of_saints_(Church_of_England)" title="Calendar of saints (Church of England)">calendar of saints</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Anglican_sacraments" title="Anglican sacraments">sacraments</a> of <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptism</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Eucharist</a> are generally thought necessary to salvation. <a href="/wiki/Infant_baptism" title="Infant baptism">Infant baptism</a> is practised. At a later age, individuals baptised as infants receive <a href="/wiki/Confirmation" title="Confirmation">confirmation</a> by a bishop, at which time they reaffirm the baptismal promises made by their parents or sponsors. The Eucharist, consecrated by a thanksgiving prayer including Christ's <a href="/wiki/Words_of_Institution" title="Words of Institution">Words of Institution</a>, is believed to be "a memorial of Christ's once-for-all redemptive acts in which Christ is objectively present and effectually received in faith".<sup id="cite_ref-EncRelg350_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EncRelg350-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>The use of hymns and <a href="/wiki/Anglican_church_music" title="Anglican church music">music in the Church of England</a> has changed dramatically over the centuries. Traditional <a href="/wiki/Evening_Prayer_(Anglican)" title="Evening Prayer (Anglican)">Choral evensong</a> is a staple of most cathedrals.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The style of <a href="/wiki/Psalm" class="mw-redirect" title="Psalm">psalm</a> chanting harks back to the Church of England's pre-reformation roots. During the 18th century, clergy such as <a href="/wiki/Charles_Wesley" title="Charles Wesley">Charles Wesley</a> introduced their own styles of worship with poetic hymns.</p>
<p>In the latter half of the 20th century, the influence of the <a href="/wiki/Charismatic_Movement" title="Charismatic Movement">Charismatic Movement</a> significantly altered the worship traditions of numerous Church of England parishes, primarily affecting those of <a href="/wiki/Evangelical" class="mw-redirect" title="Evangelical">evangelical</a> persuasion. These churches now adopt a <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_worship" title="Contemporary worship">contemporary worship</a> form of service, with minimal liturgical or ritual elements, and incorporating <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_worship_music" title="Contemporary worship music">contemporary worship music</a>.</p>
<h3><span id="Women's_ministry"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Women.27s_ministry">Women's ministry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Women's ministry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Women were appointed as deaconesses from 1861 but they could not function fully as deacons and were not considered ordained clergy. Women have been <a href="/wiki/Lay_reader" title="Lay reader">lay readers</a> for a long time. During the First World War, some women were appointed as lay readers, known as "<a href="/wiki/Bishop%27s_messenger" title="Bishop's messenger">bishop's messengers</a>", who also led missions and ran churches in the absence of men. After that no more lay readers were appointed until 1969.</p>
<p>Legislation authorising the ordination of women as deacons was passed in 1986 and they were first ordained in 1987. The <a href="/wiki/Ordination_of_women" title="Ordination of women">ordination of women</a> as priests was passed by the <a href="/wiki/General_Synod_of_the_Church_of_England" title="General Synod of the Church of England">General Synod</a> in 1992 and <a href="/wiki/List_of_the_first_32_women_ordained_as_Church_of_England_priests" title="List of the first 32 women ordained as Church of England priests">began in 1994</a>. In 2010, for the first time in the history of the Church of England, more women than men were ordained as priests (290 women and 273 men).<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>In July 2005, the synod voted to "set in train" the process of allowing the consecration of women as bishops. In February 2006, the synod voted overwhelmingly for the "further exploration" of possible arrangements for parishes that did not want to be directly under the authority of a bishop who is a woman.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> On 7 July 2008, the synod voted to approve the ordination of women as bishops and rejected moves for alternative episcopal oversight for those who do not accept the ministry of bishops who are women.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Actual ordinations of women to the episcopate required further legislation, which was narrowly rejected in a vote at General Synod in November 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 20 November 2013, the General Synod voted overwhelmingly in support of a plan to allow the ordination of women as bishops, with 378 in favour, 8 against and 25 abstentions.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 14 July 2014, the General Synod approved the ordination of women as bishops. The House of Bishops recorded 37 votes in favour, two against with one abstention. The House of Clergy had 162 in favour, 25 against and four abstentions. The House of Laity voted 152 for, 45 against with five abstentions.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> This legislation had to be approved by the Ecclesiastical Committee of the Parliament before it could be finally implemented at the November 2014 synod.</p>
<p>In December 2014, <a href="/wiki/Libby_Lane" title="Libby Lane">Libby Lane</a> was announced as the first woman to become a bishop in the Church of England. She was consecrated as a bishop in January 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>In July 2015, <a href="/wiki/Rachel_Treweek" title="Rachel Treweek">Rachel Treweek</a> was the first woman to become a diocesan bishop in the Church of England when she became the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Gloucester" title="Bishop of Gloucester">Bishop of Gloucester</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-anglicannews1_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anglicannews1-43">[43]</a></sup> She and <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Mullally" title="Sarah Mullally">Sarah Mullally</a>, Bishop of Crediton, were the first women to be ordained as bishops at <a href="/wiki/Canterbury_Cathedral" title="Canterbury Cathedral">Canterbury Cathedral</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-anglicannews1_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anglicannews1-43">[43]</a></sup> Treweek later made headlines by calling for gender-inclusive language, saying that "God is not to be seen as male. God is God."<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the consecration of the first women as bishops, <a href="/wiki/Women_and_the_Church" title="Women and the Church">Women and the Church</a> (WATCH), a group supporting the ministries of women in the Church of England, called for language referring to God as "Mother".<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> This call for more gender inclusive language has receive the outspoken support of the Rt Rev Alan Wilson, the Bishop of Buckingham.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> In 2015, the Rev Jody Stowell, from WATCH, expressed her support for female images saying "we're not restricted to understanding God with one gender. I would encourage people to explore those kinds of images. They're wholly Biblical."<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Same-sex_unions_and_LGBT_clergy">Same-sex unions and LGBT clergy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Same-sex unions and LGBT clergy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Same-sex marriage in the United Kingdom">Same-sex marriage in the United Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="LGBT rights in the United Kingdom">LGBT rights in the United Kingdom</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Homosexuality_and_the_Anglican_Communion" title="Homosexuality and the Anglican Communion">Homosexuality and the Anglican Communion</a></div>
<p>The Church of England has been discussing <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Same-sex marriage in the United Kingdom">same-sex marriages</a> and LGBT clergy. The official position, although not allowing same-sex marriage, is that "Same-sex relationships often embody genuine mutuality and fidelity."<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Within guidelines, "the law prevents ministers of the Church of England from carrying out same-sex marriages. And although there are no authorised services for blessing a same-sex civil marriage, your local church can still support you with prayer."<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> The Archbishops' Council said that "clergy in the Church of England are permitted to offer prayers of support on a pastoral basis for people in same-sex relationships;"<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> As such, many Anglican churches, with clergy open to it, "already bless same-sex couples on an unofficial basis."<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Civil_partnership_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Civil partnership in the United Kingdom">Civil Partnerships</a> for clergy have been allowed since 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-54">[54]</a></sup> By 2010, the General Synod voted in favour of extending pensions and other employee rights to clergy in civil unions.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> In a missive to clergy, the church communicated that "there was a need for committed same-sex couples to be given recognition and 'compassionate attention' from the Church, including special prayers."<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> As such, some congregations have published "Prayers for a Same Sex Commitment" as allowed within the guidelines.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> After same-sex marriage was legalised, the Archbishops' Council asked for the government to continue to offer civil unions saying "The Church of England recognises that same-sex relationships often embody fidelity and mutuality ... Civil partnerships enable these Christian virtues to be recognised socially and legally in a proper framework."<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2014, the Bishops released guidelines that permit "more informal kind of prayer" for couples.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Some congregations invite same-sex couples to receive "services of thanksgiving" after a civil marriage.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> In the guidelines, "gay couples who get married will be able to ask for special prayers in the Church of England after their wedding, the bishops have agreed."<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> In 2016, The Bishop of Grantham, the Rt Rev <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Chamberlain" title="Nicholas Chamberlain">Nicholas Chamberlain</a>, announced he is gay, in a same-sex relationship and celibate; becoming the first bishop to do so in the church.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> The church had decided in 2013 that gay clergy in <a href="/wiki/Civil_union" title="Civil union">civil partnerships</a> could become bishops.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2017, the House of Clergy voted against the motion to 'take note' of the Bishops' report defining marriage as between a man and a woman.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Due to passage in all three houses being required for passage, the motion was rejected.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> After General Synod rejected the motion, the Archbishops of Canterbury and York called for "radical new Christian inclusion" that is "based on good, healthy, flourishing relationships, and in a proper 21st century understanding of being human and of being sexual."<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Regarding transgender issues, the 2017 General Synod voted in favour of a motion saying that transgender people should be “welcomed and affirmed in their parish church...”<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> The motion also asked the Bishops "to look into special services for transgender people."<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Blackburn" title="Diocese of Blackburn">Diocese of Blackburn</a> has already begun recognising the ceremony.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Since 2000, the church has allowed priests to undergo gender transition and remain in office.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> The church has ordained openly transgender clergy since 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liberal_theology">Liberal theology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Liberal theology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Christianity" title="Liberal Christianity">Liberal Christianity</a></div>
<p>Just as the Church of England has a large conservative or "traditionalist" wing, it also has many <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Christianity" title="Liberal Christianity">liberal</a> members and clergy. Approximately one third of clergy "doubt or disbelieve in the physical resurrection".<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Others, such as the Revd <a href="/wiki/Giles_Fraser" title="Giles Fraser">Giles Fraser</a>, a contributor to <i>The Guardian</i>, have argued for an allegorical interpretation of the <a href="/wiki/Virgin_birth_of_Jesus" title="Virgin birth of Jesus">virgin birth of Jesus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> <i>The Independent</i> reported in 2014 that, according to a YouGov survey of Church of England clergy, "as many as 16 per cent are unclear about God and two per cent think it is no more than a human construct."<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Moreover, many congregations are seeker-friendly environments. For example, one report from the <a href="/wiki/Church_Mission_Society" title="Church Mission Society">Church Mission Society</a> suggested that the church open up "a pagan church where Christianity [is] very much in the centre" to reach out to spiritual people.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bioethics_issues">Bioethics issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Bioethics issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Church of England is generally opposed to abortion but recognises that "there can be - strictly limited - conditions under which it may be morally preferable to any available alternative".<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> The church also opposes euthanasia. Its official stance is that "While acknowledging the complexity of the issues involved in assisted dying/suicide and voluntary euthanasia, the Church of England is opposed to any change in the law or in medical practice that would make assisted dying/suicide or voluntary euthanasia permissible in law or acceptable in practice." It also states that "Equally, the Church shares the desire to alleviate physical and psychological suffering, but believes that assisted dying/suicide and voluntary euthanasia are not acceptable means of achieving these laudable goals."<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> However, George Carey, a former Archbishop of Canterbury, announced that he had changed his stance on euthanasia in 2014 and now advocated legalising "assisted dying".<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> On embryonic stem-cell research, the church has announced "cautious acceptance to the proposal to produce cytoplasmic hybrid embryos for research".<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Poverty">Poverty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Poverty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Church_Urban_Fund">Church Urban Fund</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Church Urban Fund">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Church_Urban_Fund" title="Church Urban Fund">Church Urban Fund</a></div>
<p>The Church of England set up the <a href="/wiki/Church_Urban_Fund" title="Church Urban Fund">Church Urban Fund</a> in the 1980s to tackle poverty and <a href="/wiki/Social_deprivation" title="Social deprivation">deprivation</a>. They see poverty as trapping individuals and communities with some people in urgent need. This leads to <a href="/wiki/Dependant" title="Dependant">dependency</a>, <a href="/wiki/Homelessness" title="Homelessness">homelessness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hunger" title="Hunger">hunger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social_isolation" title="Social isolation">isolation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Low_income" class="mw-redirect" title="Low income">low income</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mental_health" title="Mental health">mental health</a> problems, <a href="/wiki/Social_exclusion" title="Social exclusion">social exclusion</a> and violence. They feel that poverty reduces confidence and <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a> and that people born in poor conditions have difficulty escaping their disadvantaged circumstances.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Child_poverty">Child poverty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Child poverty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In parts of <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a>, Manchester and <a href="/wiki/Newcastle_upon_Tyne" title="Newcastle upon Tyne">Newcastle</a> two-thirds of babies are born to poverty and have poorer life chances, also life expectancy 15 years lower than babies born in most fortunate communities. <a href="/wiki/South_Shore,_Blackpool" title="South Shore, Blackpool">South Shore, Blackpool</a>, has lowest life expectancy at 66 years for men.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The deep-rooted unfairness in our society is highlighted by these stark statistics. Children being born in this country, just a few miles apart, couldn't witness a more wildly differing start to life. In child poverty terms, we live in one of the most unequal countries in the western world. We want people to understand where their own community sits alongside neighbouring communities. The disparity is often shocking but it's crucial that, through greater awareness, people from all backgrounds come together to think about what could be done to support those born into poverty. [Paul Hackwood, the Chair of Trustees at Church Urban Fund]<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Action_on_hunger">Action on hunger</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Action on hunger">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many prominent people in the Church of England have spoken out against poverty and welfare cuts in the United Kingdom. Twenty-seven bishops are among 43 Christian leaders who signed a letter which urged <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a> to make sure people <a href="/wiki/Hunger" title="Hunger">have enough to eat</a>.</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>We often hear talk of hard choices. Surely few can be harder than that faced by the tens of thousands of older people who must 'heat or eat' each winter, harder than those faced by families whose wages have stayed flat while food prices have gone up 30% in just five years. Yet beyond even this we must, as a society, face up to the fact that over half of people using food banks have been put in that situation by cutbacks to and failures in the benefit system, whether it be payment delays or punitive sanctions.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC20.2F02.2F14_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC20.2F02.2F14-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Welfare_state_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Welfare state in the United Kingdom">Benefit</a> cuts, failures and "punitive sanctions" force thousands of UK citizens to use <a href="/wiki/Food_bank" title="Food bank">food banks</a>. The campaign to end hunger considers this "truly shocking" and called for a national day of <a href="/wiki/Fasting" title="Fasting">fasting</a> on 4 April 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC20.2F02.2F14_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC20.2F02.2F14-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Membership">Membership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Membership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Official figures from 2005 showed there were 25 million baptised Anglicans in England and Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Due to its status as the <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">established</a> church, in general, anyone may be married, have their children baptised or their funeral in their local <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England_parish_church" title="Church of England parish church">parish church</a>, regardless of whether they are baptised or regular churchgoers.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>Between 1890 and 2001, churchgoing in the United Kingdom declined steadily.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> In the years 1968 to 1999, Anglican Sunday church attendances almost halved, from 3.5 per cent of the population to 1.9 per cent.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> By the year 2014, Sunday church attendances had declined further to 1.4 per cent of the population.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> One study published in 2008 suggested that if current trends were to continue, Sunday attendances could fall to 350,000 in 2030 and just 87,800 in 2050.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2011, the Church of England published statistics showing 1.7 million people attending at least one of its services each month, a level maintained since the turn of the millennium; approximately one million participating each Sunday and three million taking part in a Church of England service on Christmas Day or Christmas Eve. The church also claimed that 30% attend Sunday worship at least once a year; more than 40% attend a wedding in their local church and still more attend a funeral there.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Nationally the Church of England baptises one child in ten (2011).<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> In 2015, the church's statistics showed that 2.6 million people attended a special Advent service, 2.4 million attended a Christmas service, 1.3 million attended an Easter service, and 980,000 attended service during an average week.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Church of England has 18,000 active ordained clergy and 10,000 licensed lay ministers.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> In 2009, 491 people were recommended for ordination training, maintaining the level at the turn of the millennium, and 564 new clergy (266 women and 298 men) were ordained. More than half of those ordained (193 men and 116 women) were appointed to full-time paid ministry.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> In 2011, 504 new clergy were ordained, including 264 to paid ministry, and 349 <a href="/wiki/Lay_reader" title="Lay reader">lay readers</a> were admitted to ministry; and the mode age-range of those recommended for ordination training had remained 40–49 since 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Anglican_ministry" title="Anglican ministry">Anglican ministry</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Church_of_England_dioceses" title="List of Church of England dioceses">List of Church of England dioceses</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dioceses_of_Church_of_England.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Dioceses_of_Church_of_England.svg/220px-Dioceses_of_Church_of_England.svg.png" width="220" height="271" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Dioceses_of_Church_of_England.svg/330px-Dioceses_of_Church_of_England.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Dioceses_of_Church_of_England.svg/440px-Dioceses_of_Church_of_England.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="650" data-file-height="800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dioceses_of_Church_of_England.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dioceses of the Church of England
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid #00; background-color: #ff3; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Province_of_Canterbury" title="Province of Canterbury">Province of Canterbury</a></div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid #00; background-color: #f99; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Province_of_York" title="Province of York">Province of York</a></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Article XIX ('Of the Church') of the 39 Articles defines the church as follows:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Church_visible" title="Church visible">visible Church of Christ</a> is a congregation of faithful men, in which <a href="/wiki/Marks_of_the_Church" title="Marks of the Church">the pure Word of God is preached, and the sacraments be duly ministered</a> according to Christ's ordinance in all those things that of necessity are requisite to the same.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The British monarch has the constitutional title of <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Governor_of_the_Church_of_England" title="Supreme Governor of the Church of England">Supreme Governor of the Church of England</a>. The canon law of the Church of England states, "We acknowledge that the Queen's most excellent Majesty, acting according to the laws of the realm, is the highest power under God in this kingdom, and has supreme authority over all persons in all causes, as well ecclesiastical as civil."<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> In practice this power is often exercised through <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">Parliament</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Ireland" title="Church of Ireland">Church of Ireland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Church_in_Wales" title="Church in Wales">Church in Wales</a> separated from the Church of England in 1869<sup id="cite_ref-irishchurchact_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-irishchurchact-100">[100]</a></sup> and 1920<sup id="cite_ref-wales-heritage_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wales-heritage-101">[101]</a></sup> respectively and are autonomous churches in the Anglican Communion; Scotland's national church, the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a>, is <a href="/wiki/Presbyterianism" title="Presbyterianism">Presbyterian</a> but the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Episcopal_Church" title="Scottish Episcopal Church">Scottish Episcopal Church</a> is in the Anglican Communion.<sup id="cite_ref-episcopal-history_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-episcopal-history-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
