<p>In addition to England, the jurisdiction of the Church of England extends to the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Channel_Islands" title="Channel Islands">Channel Islands</a> and a few parishes in <a href="/wiki/Flintshire" title="Flintshire">Flintshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monmouthshire" title="Monmouthshire">Monmouthshire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Powys" title="Powys">Powys</a> and <a href="/wiki/Radnorshire" title="Radnorshire">Radnorshire</a> in Wales <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England_border_polls_1915%E2%80%931916" title="Church of England border polls 1915–1916">which voted to remain with the Church of England rather than joining the Church in Wales</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Expatriate congregations on the continent of Europe have become the <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Gibraltar_in_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Diocese of Gibraltar in Europe">Diocese of Gibraltar in Europe</a>.</p>
<p>The church is structured as follows (from the lowest level upwards):</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TootBaldon_StLawrence_ParishChurch.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/TootBaldon_StLawrence_ParishChurch.JPG/220px-TootBaldon_StLawrence_ParishChurch.JPG" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/TootBaldon_StLawrence_ParishChurch.JPG/330px-TootBaldon_StLawrence_ParishChurch.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/TootBaldon_StLawrence_ParishChurch.JPG/440px-TootBaldon_StLawrence_ParishChurch.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2600" data-file-height="1560" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TootBaldon_StLawrence_ParishChurch.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The parish church of St Lawrence in <a href="/wiki/Toot_Baldon" title="Toot Baldon">Toot Baldon</a> is typical of many small English village churches</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>Parish is the most local level, often consisting of one church building and community, although many parishes are joining forces in a variety of ways for financial reasons. The parish is looked after by a parish <a href="/wiki/Anglican_priest" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican priest">priest</a> who for historical or legal reasons may be called by one of the following offices: <a href="/wiki/Vicar" title="Vicar">vicar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rector_(ecclesiastical)" title="Rector (ecclesiastical)">rector</a>, <a href="/wiki/Priest_in_charge" title="Priest in charge">priest in charge</a>, team rector, team vicar. The first, second, and fourth of these may also be known as the 'incumbent'. The running of the parish is the joint responsibility of the incumbent and the <a href="/wiki/Parochial_Church_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Parochial Church Council">Parochial Church Council</a> (PCC), which consists of the parish clergy and elected representatives from the congregation. The Diocese of Gibraltar in Europe is not formally divided into parishes.</li>
<li>There are a number of local churches that do not have a parish. In urban areas there are a number of <a href="/wiki/Proprietary_chapel" title="Proprietary chapel">proprietary chapels</a> (mostly built in the 19th century to cope with urbanisation and growth in population). Also in more recent years there are increasingly <a href="/wiki/Church_plant" class="mw-redirect" title="Church plant">church plants</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fresh_expression" title="Fresh expression">fresh expressions</a> of church, whereby new congregations are planted in locations such as schools or pubs to spread the Gospel of Christ in non-traditional ways.</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Diocese_of_Gibraltar_in_Europe.PNG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Diocese_of_Gibraltar_in_Europe.PNG/220px-Diocese_of_Gibraltar_in_Europe.PNG" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Diocese_of_Gibraltar_in_Europe.PNG/330px-Diocese_of_Gibraltar_in_Europe.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Diocese_of_Gibraltar_in_Europe.PNG/440px-Diocese_of_Gibraltar_in_Europe.PNG 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="511" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Diocese_of_Gibraltar_in_Europe.PNG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map showing the Diocese of Gibraltar in Europe with the archdeaconries colour-coded</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Deanery" title="Deanery">Deanery</a>, <i>e.g.</i>, Lewisham or Runnymede. This is the area for which a <a href="/wiki/Rural_Dean" title="Rural Dean">Rural Dean</a> (or area dean) is responsible. It consists of a number of parishes in a particular district. The rural dean is usually the incumbent of one of the constituent parishes. The parishes each elect lay (non-ordained) representatives to the deanery <a href="/wiki/Synod" title="Synod">synod</a>. Deanery synod members each have a vote in the election of representatives to the diocesan synod.</li>
<li>Archdeaconry, <i>e.g.</i>, the seven in the <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Gibraltar_in_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Diocese of Gibraltar in Europe">Diocese of Gibraltar in Europe</a>. This is the area under the jurisdiction of an <a href="/wiki/Archdeacon" title="Archdeacon">archdeacon</a>. It consists of a number of deaneries.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Diocese" title="Diocese">Diocese</a>, <i>e.g.</i>, <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Durham" title="Diocese of Durham">Diocese of Durham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Guildford" title="Diocese of Guildford">Diocese of Guildford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_St_Albans" title="Diocese of St Albans">Diocese of St Albans</a>. This is the area under the jurisdiction of a diocesan bishop, <i>e.g.</i>, the Bishops of Durham, Guildford and St Albans, and will have a cathedral. There may be one or more assisting bishops, usually called <a href="/wiki/Suffragan_bishop" title="Suffragan bishop">suffragan bishops</a>, within the diocese who assist the diocesan bishop in his ministry, <i>e.g.</i>, in Guildford diocese, the Bishop of Dorking. In some very large dioceses a legal measure has been enacted to create "episcopal areas", where the diocesan bishop runs one such area himself and appoints "area bishops" to run the other areas as mini-dioceses, legally delegating many of his powers to the area bishops. Dioceses with episcopal areas include <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_London" title="Diocese of London">London</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Chelmsford" title="Diocese of Chelmsford">Chelmsford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Oxford" title="Diocese of Oxford">Oxford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Chichester" title="Diocese of Chichester">Chichester</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Diocese_of_Southwark" title="Anglican Diocese of Southwark">Southwark</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Lichfield" title="Diocese of Lichfield">Lichfield</a>. The bishops work with an elected body of <a href="/wiki/Laity" title="Laity">lay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ordained" class="mw-redirect" title="Ordained">ordained</a> representatives, known as the <a href="/wiki/Diocesan_Synod" title="Diocesan Synod">Diocesan Synod</a>, to run the diocese. A diocese is subdivided into a number of archdeaconries.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_province" title="Ecclesiastical province">Province</a>, <i>i.e.</i>, Canterbury or York. This is the area under the jurisdiction of an <a href="/wiki/Archbishop" title="Archbishop">archbishop</a>, <i>i.e.</i> the Archbishops of Canterbury and York. Decision-making within the province is the responsibility of the <a href="/wiki/General_Synod" title="General Synod">General Synod</a> (see also above). A province is subdivided into dioceses.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Primacy_of_Canterbury" title="Primacy of Canterbury">Primacy</a>, <i>i.e.</i>, Church of England. In addition to his specific authority in his own province, each archbishop is "Primate of All England" (Canterbury) or "Primate of England" (York) and has powers that extend over the whole country—for example his licence to marry without the <a href="/wiki/Banns" class="mw-redirect" title="Banns">banns</a> (marriage licence).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Royal_Peculiar" title="Royal Peculiar">Royal Peculiar</a>, a small number of churches more closely associated with <a href="/wiki/The_Crown" title="The Crown">the Crown</a>, and a very few with the law and are outside the usual church hierarchy though conforming to the rite. These are outside episcopal jurisdiction.</li>
</ul>
<p>All <a href="/wiki/Vicar#Anglican" title="Vicar">rectors and vicars</a> are <a href="/wiki/Advowson" title="Advowson">appointed by patrons</a>, who may be private individuals, corporate bodies such as cathedrals, colleges or trusts, or by the bishop or directly by the Crown. No clergy can be instituted and inducted into a parish without swearing the Oath of Allegiance to Her Majesty, and taking the Oath of Canonical Obedience "in all things lawful and honest" to the bishop. Usually they are instituted to the benefice by the bishop and then inducted by the archdeacon into the possession of the benefice property—church and parsonage. <a href="/wiki/Curate" title="Curate">Curates</a> (assistant clergy) are appointed by rectors and vicars, or if priests-in-charge by the bishop after consultation with the patron. Cathedral clergy (normally a <a href="/wiki/List_of_deans_in_the_Church_of_England" title="List of deans in the Church of England">dean</a> and a varying number of residentiary canons who constitute the cathedral chapter) are appointed either by the Crown, the bishop, or by the dean and chapter themselves. Clergy officiate in a diocese either because they hold office as beneficed clergy or are licensed by the bishop when appointed, or simply with permission.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Primates">Primates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Primates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Justin_Welby_at_Seoul_Cathedral.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Justin_Welby_at_Seoul_Cathedral.jpg/180px-Justin_Welby_at_Seoul_Cathedral.jpg" width="180" height="236" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Justin_Welby_at_Seoul_Cathedral.jpg/270px-Justin_Welby_at_Seoul_Cathedral.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Justin_Welby_at_Seoul_Cathedral.jpg/360px-Justin_Welby_at_Seoul_Cathedral.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1759" data-file-height="2308" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Justin_Welby_at_Seoul_Cathedral.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Justin_Welby" title="Justin Welby">Justin Welby</a>, <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The most senior bishop of the Church of England is the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a>, who is the <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_bishop" title="Metropolitan bishop">metropolitan</a> of the southern province of England, the Province of Canterbury. He has the status of <a href="/wiki/Primate_(bishop)" title="Primate (bishop)">Primate</a> of All England. He is the focus of unity for the worldwide Anglican Communion of independent national or regional churches. <a href="/wiki/Justin_Welby" title="Justin Welby">Justin Welby</a> has been Archbishop of Canterbury since the confirmation of his election on 4 February 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>The second most senior bishop is the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_York" title="Archbishop of York">Archbishop of York</a>, who is the metropolitan of the northern province of England, the Province of York. For historical reasons (relating to the time of York's control by the <a href="/wiki/Danelaw" title="Danelaw">Danes</a>) he is referred to as the Primate of England. <a href="/wiki/John_Sentamu" title="John Sentamu">John Sentamu</a> became Archbishop of York in 2005. The Bishop of London, the Bishop of Durham and the Bishop of Winchester are ranked in the next three positions.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diocesan_bishops">Diocesan bishops</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Diocesan bishops">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The process of appointing diocesan bishops is complex and is handled by the <a href="/wiki/Appointment_of_Church_of_England_bishops" title="Appointment of Church of England bishops">Crown Nominations Committee</a> which submits names to the Prime Minister (acting on behalf of the Crown) for consideration.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Representative_bodies">Representative bodies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Representative bodies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/General_Synod_of_the_Church_of_England" title="General Synod of the Church of England">General Synod of the Church of England</a></div>
<p>The Church of England has a legislative body, the <a href="/wiki/General_Synod_of_the_Church_of_England" title="General Synod of the Church of England">General Synod</a>. Synod can create two types of legislation, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Church_of_England_Measures" title="List of Church of England Measures">measures</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canon_law" title="Canon law">canons</a>. Measures have to be approved but cannot be amended by the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Parliament of the United Kingdom">British Parliament</a> before receiving the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Assent" class="mw-redirect" title="Royal Assent">Royal Assent</a> and becoming part of the law of England.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> Although it is the <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">established</a> church in England only, its measures must be approved by both Houses of Parliament including the non-English members. Canons require Royal Licence and Royal Assent, but form the law of the church, rather than the law of the land.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another assembly is the <a href="/wiki/Convocations_of_Canterbury_and_York" title="Convocations of Canterbury and York">Convocation of the English Clergy</a>, which is older than the General Synod and its predecessor the Church Assembly. By the 1969 Synodical Government Measure almost all of the Convocations' functions were transferred to the General Synod. Additionally, there are <a href="/wiki/Diocesan_Synod" title="Diocesan Synod">Diocesan Synods</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deanery#Anglican_usage" title="Deanery">deanery synods</a>, which are the governing bodies of the divisions of the Church.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="House_of_Lords">House of Lords</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: House of Lords">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lords_Spiritual" title="Lords Spiritual">Lords Spiritual</a></div>
<p>Of the 42 diocesan <a href="/wiki/Archbishop" title="Archbishop">archbishops</a> and bishops in the Church of England, 26 are permitted to sit in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a>. The Archbishops of <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Canterbury</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archbishops_of_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Archbishops of York">York</a> automatically have seats, as do the Bishops of <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_London" title="Bishop of London">London</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Durham" title="Bishop of Durham">Durham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Winchester" title="Bishop of Winchester">Winchester</a>. The remaining 21 seats are filled in order of seniority by <a href="/wiki/Consecration" title="Consecration">consecration</a>. It may take a diocesan bishop a number of years to reach the House of Lords, at which point he becomes a <a href="/wiki/Lords_Spiritual" title="Lords Spiritual">Lord Spiritual</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Sodor_and_Man" title="Bishop of Sodor and Man">Bishop of Sodor and Man</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Gibraltar_in_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Bishop of Gibraltar in Europe">Bishop of Gibraltar in Europe</a> are not eligible to sit in the House of Lords as their dioceses lie outside the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crown_Dependencies">Crown Dependencies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Crown Dependencies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although they are not part of <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a> or the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, the Church of England is also the <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">Established Church</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Crown_Dependencies" class="mw-redirect" title="Crown Dependencies">Crown Dependencies</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Jersey" title="Jersey">Bailiwick of Jersey</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bailiwick_of_Guernsey" title="Bailiwick of Guernsey">Bailiwick of Guernsey</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Man" title="Isle of Man">Isle of Man</a> has its own diocese of <a href="/wiki/Sodor_and_Man" class="mw-redirect" title="Sodor and Man">Sodor and Man</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Bishop_of_Sodor_and_Man" title="Bishop of Sodor and Man">Bishop of Sodor and Man</a> is an ex officio member of the <a href="/wiki/Legislative_Council_of_the_Isle_of_Man" title="Legislative Council of the Isle of Man">Legislative Council</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Tynwald" title="Tynwald">Tynwald</a> on the island.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Channel_Islands" title="Channel Islands">Channel Islands</a> are part of the <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Winchester" title="Diocese of Winchester">Diocese of Winchester</a>, and in <a href="/wiki/Jersey" title="Jersey">Jersey</a> the <a href="/wiki/Dean_of_Jersey" title="Dean of Jersey">Dean of Jersey</a> is a non-voting member of the <a href="/wiki/States_of_Jersey" title="States of Jersey">States of Jersey</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Guernsey" title="Guernsey">Guernsey</a> the Church of England is the <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">Established Church</a>, although the Dean of Guernsey is not a member of the <a href="/wiki/States_of_Guernsey" title="States of Guernsey">States of Guernsey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sex_abuse">Sex abuse</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Sex abuse">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Communion_sexual_abuse_cases" title="Anglican Communion sexual abuse cases">Anglican Communion sexual abuse cases</a></div>
<p>The Archbishop of Canterbury, <a href="/wiki/Justin_Welby" title="Justin Welby">Justin Welby</a>, has taken strong action in an effort to prevent complaints of <a href="/wiki/Anglican_Church_sexual_abuse_cases" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican Church sexual abuse cases">sex abuse cases</a> being covered up. Independent investigators are examining files as far back as the 1950s and Welby hopes this independence will prevent any possibility of a <a href="/wiki/Cover-up" title="Cover-up">cover-up</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>We will systematically bring those transparently and openly first of all working with the survivors where they are still alive and then seeing what they want. The rule is survivors come first, not our own interests, and however important the person was, however distinguished, however well-known, survivors come first. (Justin Welby)</p>
</blockquote>
<p>The personal files of all Anglican clergy since the 1950s are being audited in an effort to ensure no cover-up. Welby emphasised repeatedly that no cover-up would be acceptable.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite such assurances there is concern that not enough may be done and historic abuse may still sometimes be covered up. <a href="/wiki/Keith_Porteous_Wood" title="Keith Porteous Wood">Keith Porteous Wood</a> of the <a href="/wiki/National_Secular_Society" title="National Secular Society">National Secular Society</a> stated:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The problem wasn't that bishops weren't trained in such matters, it is the institutional culture of denial and the bullying of the abused and whistleblowers into silence. One report suggests that 13 bishops ignored letters written in the 1990s warning of abuse by Ball on behalf of a victim who later committed suicide. I have seen evidence that such bullying persists to this day. I hope that the Archbishop's review into the case of Peter Ball will deal with such bullying and what appears to be the undue influence exerted on the police and CPS by the Church in dealing with this case. The total failure of procedures, outlined by Ian Elliott, echoes that revealed in the totally damning Cahill Report about the conduct of the Archbishop Hope of York in respect of Robert Waddington. The current Archbishop of York has decided that this report should remain in printed form rather than be more widely available on the web.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Bishop <a href="/wiki/Peter_Ball_(bishop)" title="Peter Ball (bishop)">Peter Ball</a> was convicted in October 2015 on several charges of indecent assault against young adult men.<sup id="cite_ref-sentence2015_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sentence2015-113">[113]</a></sup> There are allegations of large-scale earlier cover-ups involving many British establishment figures which prevented Ball's earlier prosecution. There have also been allegations of <a href="/wiki/Child_sex_abuse" class="mw-redirect" title="Child sex abuse">child sex abuse</a>, for example <a href="/wiki/Robert_Waddington#Allegations_of_abuse" title="Robert Waddington">Robert Waddington</a>. A complainant, known only as "Joe", tried for decades to have action taken over sadistic sex abuse which Garth Moore perpetrated against him in 1976 when "Joe" was 15 years old. None of the high ranking clergy who "Joe" spoke to recall being told about the abuse, which "Joe" considers incredible.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> A representative of the solicitors firm representing "Joe" said:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The Church of England wants to bury and discourage allegations of non-recent abuse. They know how difficult it is for survivors to come forward, and it appears from this case that the Church has a plan of making it hard for these vulnerable people to come forward. This survivor has had the courage to press his case. Most do not. Most harbour the psychological fallout in silence. We need to find a way to make the system more approachable for survivors.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Financial_situation">Financial situation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Financial situation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Properties_and_finances_of_the_Church_of_England" title="Properties and finances of the Church of England">Properties and finances of the Church of England</a></div>
<p>Although an <a href="/wiki/Established_church" class="mw-redirect" title="Established church">established church</a>, the Church of England does not receive any direct government support. Donations comprise its largest source of income, and it also relies heavily on the income from its various historic endowments. In 2005, the Church of England had estimated total outgoings of around £900&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Church of England manages an <a href="/wiki/Investment_portfolio" class="mw-redirect" title="Investment portfolio">investment portfolio</a> which is worth more than £8000&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Online_church_directory">Online church directory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Online church directory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Church of England supports <i>A Church Near You</i>, an <a href="/wiki/Online" class="mw-redirect" title="Online">online</a> directory of churches. A user-edited resource, it currently lists 16,400 churches and has 7,000 editors in 42 dioceses.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> The directory enables parishes to maintain accurate location, contact and event information which is shared with other websites and <a href="/wiki/Mobile_app" title="Mobile app">mobile apps</a>. In 2012, the directory formed the data backbone of Christmas Near You<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> and in 2014 was used to promote the church's Harvest Near You initiative.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_reading">Further reading</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Church_of_England&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Further reading">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li>Garbett, Cyril, Abp. <i>The Church of England Today</i>. London: Hodder and Stoughton, 1953. 128 p.</li>
<li>Hardwick, Joseph. <i>An Anglican British world: The Church of England and the expansion of the settler empire, c. 1790–1860</i> (Manchester UP, 2014).</li>
<li>Hodges, J. P. <i>The Nature of the Lion: Elizabeth I and Our Anglican Heritage</i>. London: Faith Press, 1962. 153 pp.</li>
<li>Kirby, James. <i>Historians and the Church of England: Religion and Historical Scholarship, 1870–1920</i> (2016) online at DOI:10.1093/acprof:oso/9780198768159.001.0001</li>
<li>Lawson, Tom. <i>God and War: The Church of England and Armed Conflict in the Twentieth Century</i> (Routledge, 2016).</li>
<li>Maughan Steven S. <i>Mighty England Do Good: Culture, Faith, Empire, and World in the Foreign Missions of the Church of England, 1850–1915</i> (2014)</li>
<li>Picton, Hervé. <i>A Short History of the Church of England: From the Reformation to the Present Day</i>. Newcastle upon Tyne: Cambridge Scholars Publishing, 2015. 180 p.</li>
<li>Rowlands, John Henry Lewis. <i>Church, State, and Society, 1827–1845: the Attitudes of John Keble, Richard Hurrell Froude, and John Henry Newman</i>. (1989). xi, 262 p. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1-85093-132-1" title="Special:BookSources/1-85093-132-1">1-85093-132-1</a></li>
<li>Tapsell, Grant. <i>The later Stuart Church, 1660–1714</i> (2012).</li>
</ul>
